  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_949f0976-fee9-415d-931a-04b72b8560c8'
      });

  <span id="freeText9057153483428482825" style="display:none"><i>'What's so hard about pulling a sword out of a stone? The real work's already been done. You ought to make yourself useful and find the man who put the sword in the stone in the first place.'</i><br /><br />The City Watch needs MEN! But what it's got includes Corporal Carrot (technically a dwarf), Lance-constable Cuddy (really a dwarf), Lance-constable Detritus (a troll), Lance-constable Angua (a woman... most of the time) and Corporal Nobbs (disqualified from the human race for shoving).<br /><br />And they need all the help they can get, because someone in Ankh-Morpork has been getting dangerous ideas - about crowns and legendary swords, and destiny. And the problem with destiny is, of course, that she is not always careful where she points her finger. One minute you might be minding your own business on a normal if not spectacular career path, the next you might be in the frame for the big job, like saving the world...</span>
  <a data-text-id="9057153483428482825" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>
</div>

<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">I have to say no.<br/>Younger adults will probably enjoy it, but the literary and social commentary will probably go over their heads. <br/>Think of this as a f<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">I have to say no.<br />Younger adults will probably enjoy it, but the literary and social commentary will probably go over their heads. <br />Think of this as a fantasy noir book.<br /><br />Having said that I gave this to a 12 y/o friend who raced through it and came back for the rest of the series....<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer10760968357240324971">“The reason that the rich were so rich, Vimes reasoned, was because they managed to spend less money.<br/><br/>Take boots, for example. He earned thirty-eight dollars a month plus allowances. A really good pair of leather boots cost fifty dollars. But an affordable pair of boots, which were sort of OK for a season or two and then leaked like hell when the cardboard gave out, cost about ten dollars. Those were the kind of boots Vimes always bought, and wore until the soles were so thin that he could tell w</span>
  <span id="freeText10760968357240324971" style="display:none">“The reason that the rich were so rich, Vimes reasoned, was because they managed to spend less money.<br /><br />Take boots, for example. He earned thirty-eight dollars a month plus allowances. A really good pair of leather boots cost fifty dollars. But an affordable pair of boots, which were sort of OK for a season or two and then leaked like hell when the cardboard gave out, cost about ten dollars. Those were the kind of boots Vimes always bought, and wore until the soles were so thin that he could tell where he was in Ankh-Morpork on a foggy night by the feel of the cobbles.<br /><br />But the thing was that good boots lasted for years and years. A man who could afford fifty dollars had a pair of boots that'd still be keeping his feet dry in ten years' time, while the poor man who could only afford cheap boots would have spent a hundred dollars on boots in the same time and would still have wet feet.<br /><br />This was the Captain Samuel Vimes 'Boots' theory of socioeconomic unfairness.” <br /><br /><br />Things like this aren't the only reason I love Terry Pratchett, but they're one of the main reasons. </span>

<div id="review_2320523468" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2320523468" />
    <a title="Mario the lone bookwolf (semi reviewing hiatus ) " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/78485297-mario-the-lone-bookwolf-semi-reviewing-hiatus"><img alt="Mario the lone bookwolf (semi reviewing hiatus ) " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588320656p2/78485297.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2320523468?book_show_action=true">Mar 07, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mario the lone bookwolf (semi reviewing hiatus ) " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mario the lone bookwolf (semi reviewing hiatus ) " href="/user/show/78485297-mario-the-lone-bookwolf-semi-reviewing-hiatus">Mario the lone bookwolf (semi reviewing hiatus ) </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/78485297-mario-the-lone-bookwolf-semi-reviewing-hiatus?shelf=pratchett-terry">pratchett-terry</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2320523468" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3860502796035076920">Quota policy, by integrating minorities in the already chaotic and dysfunctional City Watch, while a military arms race and intrigues are boiling.<br/><br/>Prejudices and racism are some of the main tropes behind the curtain and this often used elements come to ingenious culminations with the integration of alternative fighters from contrasting fantasy folks. Using the different magic abilities, weaknesses, and natural hereditary enmities as metaphors, the City Watch turns into a multicultural melting pot</span>
  <span id="freeText3860502796035076920" style="display:none">Quota policy, by integrating minorities in the already chaotic and dysfunctional City Watch, while a military arms race and intrigues are boiling.<br /><br />Prejudices and racism are some of the main tropes behind the curtain and this often used elements come to ingenious culminations with the integration of alternative fighters from contrasting fantasy folks. Using the different magic abilities, weaknesses, and natural hereditary enmities as metaphors, the City Watch turns into a multicultural melting pot of ideas, stereotypes, and vendettas. <br /><br />I am not sure how many dark fantasy comedy detective novels could be seen as heirs of this idea of investigating trolls, werewolves, vampires, zombies, whatevers,…, but the potential of using the magic abilities for finding the nasty criminals has great potential, as it unites plot, character, and ability and enables manifold expansion options. As far as I know, Butcher did it best with his close to legendary Dresden Files series and I am too lazy to remember and name similar authors.<br /><br />These two elements, open and hidden racism, the different abilities, secret superweapons, another evil force in the background next to Vetinari and, jay, a wedding, how romantic, make it one of Pratchett's best works. He is now at the peak of his craft, took some books to really get started and become more professional, and will, from the novels of this middle, golden age period on be one of the best, or even the best, humoristic author. Until he turns so dark that I still remember some of the extremely depressing and sad scenes he used in his later work.<br /><br />Carrot is big in this, his subtle, Vetinari style cool badassness is urgently needed to handle the situation. I didn´t look enough for the innuendos and connotations surrounding Carrot´s secret and how it plays with the philosophy of good leadership and responsibility, I have to take a more detailed glimpse at it in the big reread.<br /><br />I also don´t remember enough about the implications, innuendos, and gags regarding militarization and how new technology can be used, abused, and misused and how Pratchett instrumentalized one of the most defining elements of human history to satirize greed and misled patriotism. Technologies and ideologies and their influence on more or less primitive societies are an often seen element of his work, something reality should possibly cut a slice off from, because these two little critters are often underestimated, or simply overseen, harbingers of prosperity or doom.<br /><br />Tropes show how literature is conceptualized and created and which mixture of elements makes works and genres unique: <br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://tvtropes.org/pmwiki/pmwiki.php/Literature/MenAtArms">https://tvtropes.org/pmwiki/pmwiki.ph...</a><br /><br />This one is added to all Pratchettian reviews:<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scheibenwelt-Romane#Liste_der_Romane">https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scheibe...</a> <br />The idea of the dissected motifs rocks, highlighting the main real world inspirational elements of fiction and satire is something usually done with so called higher literature, but a much more interesting field in readable literature, as it offers the joy of reading, subtle criticism, and feeling smart all together.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3860502796035076920" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/61530057?book_show_action=true">Jun 29, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Patrick" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Patrick" href="/user/show/922495-patrick">Patrick</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/922495-patrick?shelf=books-i-would-blurb">books-i-would-blurb</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer61530057" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14549409743010965691">This is the second book in the City Watch storyline in the Discworld novels. And I have to say, it's probably twice as good as Guards Guards.<br/><br/>All the characters are more fully realized and more compelling. What's more, it's obvious to me reading now that Pratchett has multi-book plans for the central characters: Carrot, Vimes, and Angua. <br/><br/>Detrius also has a pretty strong secondary arc in this one, and we see the begining of some of Pratchett's Troll Vs. Dwarf discussions that come to beautiful fru</span>
  <span id="freeText14549409743010965691" style="display:none">This is the second book in the City Watch storyline in the Discworld novels. And I have to say, it's probably twice as good as Guards Guards.<br /><br />All the characters are more fully realized and more compelling. What's more, it's obvious to me reading now that Pratchett has multi-book plans for the central characters: Carrot, Vimes, and Angua. <br /><br />Detrius also has a pretty strong secondary arc in this one, and we see the begining of some of Pratchett's Troll Vs. Dwarf discussions that come to beautiful fruition in Thud.<br /><br />Carrot still a focal character here, and he's interesting, charming, all that. But Vimes is consistently stealing the show. He's the Batman to Carrot's Superman.<br /><br />Given that comparison, it's not surprising that Vimes keeps our attention more. Perfect characters just aren't compelling in the same way.<br /><br />Part of me wishes that Angua's character and plot was bigger. But that's just fiddling and griping. Not every book can have everything. And this book has so much more than most. <br /><br />Excellent read, absolutely worth your time.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14549409743010965691" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_976479756" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/976479756" />
    <a title="Brandon Sanderson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3145554-brandon-sanderson"><img alt="Brandon Sanderson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1394044413p2/3145554.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/976479756?book_show_action=true">Jun 24, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brandon Sanderson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brandon Sanderson" href="/user/show/3145554-brandon-sanderson">Brandon Sanderson</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer976479756" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8347683339037189586">(This review is from 2006.)<br/><br/>All right, the short of it is I really liked the book. The long of it is, I’m very annoyed at Terry.<br/><br/>Those of you who have been following things here know that I just sold the <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/623976.Alcatraz_Versus_the_Evil_Librarians__Alcatraz___1_' title='Alcatraz Versus the Evil Librarians (Alcatraz, #1) by Brandon Sanderson' rel='nofollow noopener'>Alcatraz</a> books to Scholastic. They’re essentially humorous fantasy—evil librarians running the world and all that. I wrote them because I was a little frustrated at the market. I could find funny books (<a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/36746.Lemony_Snicket' title='Lemony Snicket' rel='nofollow noopener'>Snicket</a>) and I could find books with good worldbuilding (<a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3618.Philip_Pullman' title='Philip Pullman' rel='nofollow noopener'>Pullman</a>) and I could find books with c</span>
  <span id="freeText8347683339037189586" style="display:none">(This review is from 2006.)<br /><br />All right, the short of it is I really liked the book. The long of it is, I’m very annoyed at Terry.<br /><br />Those of you who have been following things here know that I just sold the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/623976.Alcatraz_Versus_the_Evil_Librarians__Alcatraz___1_" title="Alcatraz Versus the Evil Librarians (Alcatraz, #1) by Brandon Sanderson" rel="nofollow noopener">Alcatraz</a> books to Scholastic. They’re essentially humorous fantasy—evil librarians running the world and all that. I wrote them because I was a little frustrated at the market. I could find funny books (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/36746.Lemony_Snicket" title="Lemony Snicket" rel="nofollow noopener">Snicket</a>) and I could find books with good worldbuilding (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3618.Philip_Pullman" title="Philip Pullman" rel="nofollow noopener">Pullman</a>) and I could find books with clever pacing and plotting (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1077326.J_K__Rowling" title="J.K. Rowling" rel="nofollow noopener">Rowling</a>).<br /><br />What I couldn’t find was a series that had both amusing text AND engaging characters. They all seemed to sacrifice one for the other—which is fine. After all, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4.Douglas_Adams" title="Douglas Adams" rel="nofollow noopener">Douglas Adams</a> didn’t give much of a hoot for compelling characters, and look where he went. Still, I wanted something with both. And, as it turns out, I happen to write books for a living. Hence, the ALCATRAZ books were born.<br /><br />Turns out that Terry has been doing this for years. At least, he has been if <i>Men at Arms</i> is a good example. (Note—I realized all of my examples above were YA, and this is adult, but if you can’t make a forced metaphor once in a while, then what’s the good of having a literary license in the first place?)<br /><br />Anyway, I thoroughly enjoyed the book—perhaps as much as everyone told me that I would. Now, my Pratchett experience is limited. I tried <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/34497.The_Color_of_Magic__Discworld___1_" title="The Color of Magic (Discworld, #1) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">The Color of Magic</a> when I was younger (I’ve since learned that it is a poor representation of the series) and have read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12067.Good_Omens_The_Nice_and_Accurate_Prophecies_of_Agnes_Nutter__Witch" title="Good Omens The Nice and Accurate Prophecies of Agnes Nutter, Witch by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Good Omens</a> (which was brilliant, and which ultimately led me to give Discworld another go.)<br /><br />I guess the reason I liked <i>Men at Arms</i> was that balance. It was funny—and not in a cheap way either. It was funny in a clever, scholarly, satire sort of way, with an occasional bad pun or lowbrow shot to keep you on your toes. But, somehow, Pratchett still managed to make me care a great deal about his characters. (Thereby stealing my great, wonderfully original idea for the ALCATRAZ books—that of giving people character arcs.)<br /><br />How well Terry did this is still a little dumbfounding to me. All of his characters seemed pretty single-sided at the beginning. And, they didn’t really get that much deeper as the story progressed. Yet, they became irresistible.<br /><br />Good tension in books is based, in my opinion, on making the reader care about the characters. Any book will feel fast paced if the characters are in danger. And, Terry is obviously a very good craftsman, with excellent pacing beyond his character drama.<br /><br />So, anyway, it’s a fine book. If you, like me, have been living in a hole and ignoring Pratchett, then this is a good one with which to start.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8347683339037189586" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer101060297" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7478497053792648124">In the last few weeks the world has not been my friend, among some health issues and a recent string of mediocre books. In times like these, Terry Pratchett is a wonderful antidote to the world’s unpleasantness. <br/><br/>So a fair warning: this review will be yet another love letter to Discworld and his creator Sir Terry Pratchett. There my be some fangirl gushing. Read at your own risk.<br/><blockquote> <img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1603562205i/30283262._SX540_.jpg' width='400' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/>
</blockquote><br/>————<br/>To me, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/400354.Men_at_Arms__Discworld___15__City_Watch__2_' title='Men at Arms (Discworld, #15; City Watch #2) by Terry Pratchett' rel='nofollow noopener'>Men at Arms</a> has always been a bit different from your usual Pratchett’s City Watch books. You see, as Ankh</span>
  <span id="freeText7478497053792648124" style="display:none">In the last few weeks the world has not been my friend, among some health issues and a recent string of mediocre books. In times like these, Terry Pratchett is a wonderful antidote to the world’s unpleasantness. <br><br>So a fair warning: this review will be yet another love letter to Discworld and his creator Sir Terry Pratchett. There my be some fangirl gushing. Read at your own risk.<br><blockquote> <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1603562205i/30283262._SX540_.jpg" width="400" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</blockquote><br>————<br>To me, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/400354.Men_at_Arms__Discworld___15__City_Watch__2_" title="Men at Arms (Discworld, #15; City Watch #2) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Men at Arms</a> has always been a bit different from your usual Pratchett’s City Watch books. You see, as Ankh-Morkpork City Watch subcycle progresses, from <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/64216.Guards__Guards___Discworld___8__City_Watch__1_" title="Guards! Guards! (Discworld, #8; City Watch #1) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Guards! Guards!</a> to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8785374.Snuff__Discworld___39__City_Watch__8_" title="Snuff (Discworld, #39; City Watch #8) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Snuff</a>, the spotlight gets focused more and more on Sam Vimes, the Watch Commander and a noble by marriage, coming from humble beginnings and (often literally) the gutter, the sarcastic and gruff and unfailingly just, with all illusions about the world stripped away by life, leaving deep cynicism and excellent understanding of the small unpleasantnesses that drive human nature. But <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/400354.Men_at_Arms__Discworld___15__City_Watch__2_" title="Men at Arms (Discworld, #15; City Watch #2) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Men at Arms</a>, the second in the Watch series, is a bit different from the rest. <b>It’s not much of a Vimes book. No, it’s Captain Carrot’s book, his moment in the sun.</b><br><blockquote>
  <i> “Sometimes it’s better to light a flamethrower than curse the darkness.” </i>
</blockquote><br>When we met Carrot in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/64216.Guards__Guards___Discworld___8__City_Watch__1_" title="Guards! Guards! (Discworld, #8; City Watch #1) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Guards! Guards!</a>, he was naive and simple. A six-foot-tall dwarf (he’s adopted) and - as everyone knows but mostly does not say - a true heir to the ancient throne of Ankh-Morpork, he came to the Watch as a very young and astonishingly naive recruit, believing in life by the rules, with very strong sense of right and wrong and justice, with ample charisma that further underscored his apparent simplicity. But it was then. Now, in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/400354.Men_at_Arms__Discworld___15__City_Watch__2_" title="Men at Arms (Discworld, #15; City Watch #2) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Men at Arms</a>, Carrot gets his moment, <b>his chance to show why, along with Vimes, he is the heart and soul of the Watch</b>. This is Pratchett’s apology to Carrot for making him a bit of a buffoon in the previous story. <br><br><b>His wide-eyed simplicity turns out to have layers and a few unexpectedly sharp corners. </b><blockquote>
  <i>“Colon thought Carrot was simple. Carrot often struck people as simple. And he was.<br>Where people went wrong was thinking that simple meant the same thing as stupid.”</i>
</blockquote><blockquote> <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1603562206i/30283263._SX540_.jpg" width="400" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</blockquote>Sam Vimes is set to leave the Watch after his impending nuptials to Lady Sybil Ramkin, the noblest and richest woman in Ankh-Morpork (<i>“The Ramkins were more highly bred than a hilltop bakery, whereas Corporal Nobbs had been disqualified from the human race for shoving.”</i>). <b>All while the Watch expands and diversifies - now they employ a troll, a dwarf, and a w- .... ummm, let’s just say a woman</b> - while a string of suspicious murders with a strange new weapon eventually known as a <i>gonne</i> <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ <blockquote> <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1603562206i/30283264._SX540_.jpg" width="400" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</blockquote>  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> occur - and ethnic tensions between dwarfs and trolls intensify, including in the Watch, and <b>someone needs to sort it all out.</b><br><blockquote>
  <i> “Murder was in fact a fairly uncommon event in Ankh-Morpork, but there were a lot of suicides. Walking in the night-time alleyways of The Shades was suicide. Asking for a short in a dwarf bar was suicide. Saying 'Got rocks in your head?' to a troll was suicide. You could commit suicide very easily, if you weren't careful.” </i>
</blockquote><br>Enter Carrot Ironfoundersson, a six-foot dwarf and a rightful (although mostly secret) heir to the throne, with his charisma and lack of sarcasm or subtlety, and overwhelming sincerity, and an undeniable attraction to Corporal Angua, a new recruit who is a woman AND a werewolf (a vegetarian who works very hard to not become, well, a humanitarian).<br><br>And far from being silly, Carrot’s strict moral code and a sharp unyielding sense of justice are impossible not to admire. <b>It’s him after all who utters one of the most profound statements that should - but sadly don’t - underscore how one should live life:</b><blockquote> <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1603562206i/30283265._SX540_.jpg" width="400" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</blockquote><blockquote>
  <i>“Personal isn't the same as important.”</i>
</blockquote><br>And of course I love it. Every sentence and every word.<br><br>For a rather short and humorously satirical book, this one in Pratchett’s trademark manner delves into pretty deep subject matters (responsibility, discrimination, ethnic tensions, gun control, just to name a few), peeks into the unpleasant recesses of human (or humanoid) soul, <b>and while holding a mirror to the unsettling bits of ourselves still manages easily and seemingly effortlessly to shine with hope and kindness and sarcasm-tinged deep understanding of humanity</b>. That’s quintessential Terry Pratchett for you.<br><blockquote>
  <i>“The reason that the rich were so rich, Vimes reasoned, was because they managed to spend less money. Take boots, for example. He earned thirty-eight dollars a month plus allowances. A really good pair of leather boots cost fifty dollars. But an affordable pair of boots, which were sort of OK for a season or two and then leaked like hell when the cardboard gave out, cost about ten dollars. Those were the kind of boots Vimes always bought, and wore until the soles were so thin that he could tell where he was in Ankh-Morpork on a foggy night by the feel of the cobbles. But the thing was that good boots lasted for years and years. A man who could afford fifty dollars had a pair of boots that’d still be keeping his feet dry in ten years’ time, while a poor man who could only afford cheap boots would have spent a hundred dollars on boots in the same time and would still have wet feet. This was the Captain Samuel Vimes “Boots” theory of socioeconomic unfairness.”</i>
</blockquote> <blockquote> <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1603562441i/30283273._SX540_.jpg" width="400" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</blockquote>One of the absolute highlights of this book is a slow budding friendship between the traditionally sworn enemies, a dwarf (Cuddy) and a troll (Detritus). It’s interesting to see the slowly unraveling of ingrained stereotypes, the tentative building of trust and respect, the learning to look beyond the superficial - appearance, ethnicity - and <b>discard the focus on differences and instead see and understand the individual underneath it all - a friend, a colleague, a *person*.</b> Pratchett is amazing and making you see the bigger whole instead of smaller insignificant parts, and I absolutely love it.<br><br>And of course, as always, he underscores the power and the need to think for yourself instead of blindly following the mob, or a charismatic leader.<br><blockquote>
  <i> “People ought to think for themselves, Captain Vimes says. The problem is, people only think for themselves if you tell them to.”</i>
</blockquote><br>Needless to say, I love the wit and humor of this book. The puns, the humorous footnotes, the jokes - when Pratchett’s writing is involved I never find them overused or tiresome.<br><blockquote>
  <i>“Besides, when you hit your thumb with an eight-pound hammer it’s nice to be able to blaspheme. It takes a very special and strong-minded kind of atheist to jump up and down with their hand clasped under their other armpit and shout, “Oh, random fluctuations-in-the-space-time-contiuum!” or “Aaargh, primitive-and-outmoded-concept on a crutch!” </i>
</blockquote><br><b>Unquestionable 5 stars. My hope in this world is partially restored.</b><br><br>————<br>My ever-expanding collection of Pratchett’s Discworld reviews:<br>- <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/101060202" rel="nofollow noopener">Guards! Guards!</a> <br>- <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/101060297" rel="nofollow noopener">Men at Arms</a><br>- <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/101060304" rel="nofollow noopener">Thud!</a><br>- <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/157609988" rel="nofollow noopener">Lords and Ladies</a><br>- <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/160703976" rel="nofollow noopener">The Wee Free Men</a><br>- <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/101060281" rel="nofollow noopener">Hogfather</a><br>- <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/312984829" rel="nofollow noopener">Monstrous Regiment</a></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>
  <a data-text-id="7478497053792648124" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

    <a title="Lyn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5253785-lyn"><img alt="Lyn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1495541962p2/5253785.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1924509595?book_show_action=true">Mar 02, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lyn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lyn" href="/user/show/5253785-lyn">Lyn</a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1924509595" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10786677631357912443">Was Terry Pratchett the English Kurt Vonnegut?<br/><br/>Here is another example of playful satire that thinly hides a stinging social and cultural admonishment. In his 1993 Discworld novel Men at Arms (the 15th Discworld adventure and the second to feature Sam Vimes and his City Watch crew) Sir Terry tackles such heavy subjects as racism, sexism, political correctness, class distinctions and the inhumanity of marshal technology but in a decidedly not-too-heavy format; impishly mocking what needs mocking a</span>
  <span id="freeText10786677631357912443" style="display:none">Was Terry Pratchett the English Kurt Vonnegut?<br><br>Here is another example of playful satire that thinly hides a stinging social and cultural admonishment. In his 1993 Discworld novel Men at Arms (the 15th Discworld adventure and the second to feature Sam Vimes and his City Watch crew) Sir Terry tackles such heavy subjects as racism, sexism, political correctness, class distinctions and the inhumanity of marshal technology but in a decidedly not-too-heavy format; impishly mocking what needs mocking and throwing down not the gauntlet but instead the soft mitten.<br><br>Discworld fans will love to see the return of the City Watch along with a deterministically diverse set of new recruits, demonstrating Ankh-Morpork’s commitment to employment equality. In Ankh-Morpork, a multi-cultural, pluralistic, metropolitan city if ever there was one, humans live together with trolls, dwarfs and any number of other kinds of folk and Pratchett waxes poetic about the strengths of diversity but at the barstool rather than the pulpit.<br><br>We also get to know The Patrician better and really a winning element of this book is Pratchett’s mature and qualitative characterization. We knew these players before, but in these pages, Pratchett provides more illuminating introductions – like getting to know acquaintances and becoming better friends.<br><br>Discworld seems to be to Pratchett what Mars was to Bradbury: his vehicle for metaphor and allegory, the canvas for his brush, the macaroni to his cheese. In Men at Arms, like so many of these enjoyable Discworld escapades, what goes on atop Great A’Tuin is in periphery of what goes on in our minds, an idyllic statement about how things should be, or at least as they would be if more fun.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1547042882i/26891348.jpg" width="400" height="333" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
  <a data-text-id="10786677631357912443" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer571288420" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer10232105480451257471">Discworld Re-Read project #15. :)<br/><br/>I remembered that there was one particular Watch novel that lunged the entire Watch novels out of the stratosphere in terms of how much I grew to LOVE them. I had forgotten that THIS was that novel.<br/><br/>Vimes was great, but who really stole the show was Carrot. I'll love Vimes a lot more in the future, but for now, Carrot is KING.<br/><br/>Or not. That's a matter of perception and some small debate, all of which Carrot himself will probably have the right precedent and moral ou</span>
  <span id="freeText10232105480451257471" style="display:none">Discworld Re-Read project #15. :)<br /><br />I remembered that there was one particular Watch novel that lunged the entire Watch novels out of the stratosphere in terms of how much I grew to LOVE them. I had forgotten that THIS was that novel.<br /><br />Vimes was great, but who really stole the show was Carrot. I'll love Vimes a lot more in the future, but for now, Carrot is KING.<br /><br />Or not. That's a matter of perception and some small debate, all of which Carrot himself will probably have the right precedent and moral outlook and word to set right.<br /><br />Other than that, this novel deals with racial prejudice in a big way. Trolls and Dwarves are at each other's throats. And then the Assassin's guild is deep in the muck thanks to a little theft and ideology. And then there's Gaspode. <br /><br />I don't think there's any part of the novel I disliked. At all. Wolves and dogs and romance and bringing back the old monarchy kinda reverberated with a previous novel, of course, but I didn't mind. This was a different kind of beast. This time there was rioting in the streets rather than dragons. :)<br /><br />Too cool. :)</span>

<div id="review_1994269146" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1994269146" />
    <a title="Mort" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/67602782-mort"><img alt="Mort" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1586932919p2/67602782.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1994269146?book_show_action=true">May 08, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mort" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mort" href="/user/show/67602782-mort">Mort</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/67602782-mort?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1994269146" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2456887560725420431">5 STARS<br/>Fact: I don’t like to read fantasy.<br/>Fact: The very idea of reading a book without chapters seems like hard work.<br/>Fact: I own more than 40 Terry Pratchett novels.<br/>Fact: I have read every Discworld novel at least twice.<br/><br/>Why, you ask?<br/><br/>Quite simply: Terry Pratchett was the funniest writer in the world!<br/><br/>I discovered him by accident. While visiting family one day, I had 15 minutes to kill, so I grabbed the only book I could see. The cover looked somewhat juvenile, but it beats being bored. Two hours </span>
  <span id="freeText2456887560725420431" style="display:none">5 STARS<br />Fact: I don’t like to read fantasy.<br />Fact: The very idea of reading a book without chapters seems like hard work.<br />Fact: I own more than 40 Terry Pratchett novels.<br />Fact: I have read every Discworld novel at least twice.<br /><br />Why, you ask?<br /><br />Quite simply: Terry Pratchett was the funniest writer in the world!<br /><br />I discovered him by accident. While visiting family one day, I had 15 minutes to kill, so I grabbed the only book I could see. The cover looked somewhat juvenile, but it beats being bored. Two hours later I was thinking of excuses why I had to borrow the book.<br /><br />I can’t write reviews for all his books, because the Discworld series is much too dear to me – they all deserve 5 stars (or more)! His main characters are so well described and human, I can usually place someone I know or have met before in their shoes, or I’ll find myself meeting someone and thinking: This person reminds me of…<br /><br />I laughed my ass off in all the Discworld novels and, if I gave it about two years between readings, I would laugh even harder the second time I read it.<br /><br />Alzheimer’s took Sir Terry Pratchett from us, and it was a loss for our world. My wife told me when I heard the news, it was almost like a death in our own family.<br /><br />Today I’d like to think that he’s having a ball wherever he may be – no glands to worry about – and maybe even playing the odd practical joke on DEATH.<br /><br />RIP, good Sir, you made the world a better place while you were here.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2456887560725420431" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer5768135421137868276">So, what do I think ? Well, when I started (in Jan 19) (re)reading these Discworld novels in order (it had been some years since I last read any of them) I started remembering characters from my earlier reads. I remember thinking " oh Rincewind is just excellent", then it was" oh but the three witches are so funny", and of course" DEATH is the best character, and then as you move through the books, you come across the Night Watch and you suddenly realise that they are your new favourite.<br/><br/>This boo</span>
  <span id="freeText5768135421137868276" style="display:none">So, what do I think ? Well, when I started (in Jan 19) (re)reading these Discworld novels in order (it had been some years since I last read any of them) I started remembering characters from my earlier reads. I remember thinking " oh Rincewind is just excellent", then it was" oh but the three witches are so funny", and of course" DEATH is the best character, and then as you move through the books, you come across the Night Watch and you suddenly realise that they are your new favourite.<br /><br />This book was just so well written, and the characters in the Night Watch including the new recruits are so well formed after even only a couple of ages. The existing NW characters are of course like old friends, Vimes, Carrot and Nobby, and they are also joined by Gaspode the talking dog and a couple of cameo appearances from CMOT Dibbler.<br /><br />A great story with a worthy end, and truly deserving of the 5 stars.</span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2477197793" />
    <a title="Trish" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3461034-trish"><img alt="Trish" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588663568p2/3461034.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2477197793?book_show_action=true">Sep 01, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Trish" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Trish" href="/user/show/3461034-trish">Trish</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audiobook 9780753122563" href="/book/show/1141284.Men_at_Arms">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2477197793" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17539197998286568578">Holy ... this was ... blimey!<br/><br/>I'm used to an awesome level of brilliance by Sir Terry but this novel might have outdone everything I've read about the Discworld so far!<br/><br/>We're back in Ankh-Morpork in this installment, in time for Sam Vimes' wedding to Lady Sibyl (she who breeds dragons). This also means that his retirement is at hand. For a career copper, you can imagine what that prospect means.<br/>Moreover, the Night Watch has increased its ranks slightly thanks to the Patrician's inclusion initiativ</span>
  <span id="freeText17539197998286568578" style="display:none">Holy ... this was ... blimey!<br><br>I'm used to an awesome level of brilliance by Sir Terry but this novel might have outdone everything I've read about the Discworld so far!<br><br>We're back in Ankh-Morpork in this installment, in time for Sam Vimes' wedding to Lady Sibyl (she who breeds dragons). This also means that his retirement is at hand. For a career copper, you can imagine what that prospect means.<br>Moreover, the Night Watch has increased its ranks slightly thanks to the Patrician's inclusion initiative so we have Detritus the troll, Cuddy the dwarf and Angua who is a human female (most of the time) reinforcing Sgts. Colon, Nobby and Carrot.<br>When there is a string of murders, the Patrician explicitly forbids Vimes from investigating, starting some awesome police work, wonderful etymological history lessons and character studies as dark as a moonless night (please don't argue that moonless nights aren't all that dark thanks to the stars, Captain Vimes already had that discussion). Oh, and the city needs to be prevented from tearing itself apart because you know what they say about ...<br><br>Seriously, I was NOT prepared for <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Cuddy actually dying <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> and was VERY emotional about it! Both new pairs (Carrot and Angua as well as Detritus and Cuddy) were a banter-filled delight. I actually liked the troll-dwarf combo more than even my beloved Gaspode who also features prominently here.<br>If I had to name a favourite character, I honestly couldn't. Honest and generous Vimes, good-hearted Carrot, adorably smart Gaspode, Detritus, Cuddy, ... even Colon and Nobby or a certain psychotic poodle. Most of all, many of these shine when they are thrown in together (such as the Patrician with Carrot) and I love them all dearly. Just like the city itself with all its guilds and rules.<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1535797817i/26234566.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>The humour is dark, the action breathtaking, the characters lively. I laughed and cried almost through the entire book and was once again astonished how on-point the author was about current events and human nature (especially considering the novel's age).<br>Once again, the tragedy as well as the humour came in big and small moments (one small one being when DEATH tried to make a certain person's death "more enjoyable" *lol*).<br><br>Moreover, this definitely served as the gateway to making The Watch into what it is and opening up greater possibilities, giving us readers the promise of many more slap-stick adventures.<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1535797817i/26234567._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>
  <a data-text-id="17539197998286568578" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer8397149893957169308">This was a reread - the last time I read it was so long ago I don't remember:) <br/><br/>Such a good book! This man only wrote good books and he is much missed. <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/61651.Men_at_Arms__Discworld___15__City_Watch___2_' title='Men at Arms (Discworld, #15; City Watch, #2) by Terry Pratchett' rel='nofollow noopener'>Men at Arms</a> has to be a hit with me because it contains so many of my favourite characters. There is Corporal Carrot who was adopted as a child by dwarves but is probably the disinherited King of Ankh-Morpork. He has so much charisma he changes the world just by being in it. Captain Vimes is there too, about to get married and leave the Force and </span>
  <span id="freeText8397149893957169308" style="display:none">This was a reread - the last time I read it was so long ago I don't remember:) <br /><br />Such a good book! This man only wrote good books and he is much missed. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/61651.Men_at_Arms__Discworld___15__City_Watch___2_" title="Men at Arms (Discworld, #15; City Watch, #2) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Men at Arms</a> has to be a hit with me because it contains so many of my favourite characters. There is Corporal Carrot who was adopted as a child by dwarves but is probably the disinherited King of Ankh-Morpork. He has so much charisma he changes the world just by being in it. Captain Vimes is there too, about to get married and leave the Force and not happy about it. Veterinari plays a delightful role and even manages to make a mistake and get shot which is a first. And then of course there is Death, one of Pratchett's greatest ever characters. You have to read the books to appreciate why.<br /><br />To enjoy these books you have to like the type of humour. I love it and could easily read the whole series again. In fact I probably will!!!</span>

<div id="review_92051287" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/92051287" />
    <a title="Steven Harbin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1283483-steven-harbin"><img alt="Steven Harbin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1307406902p2/1283483.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/92051287?book_show_action=true">Mar 01, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steven Harbin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steven Harbin" href="/user/show/1283483-steven-harbin">Steven Harbin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback " href="/book/show/16067097-men-at-arms">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">people who like fantasy, humor, satire</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer92051287" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer365325837735762315">I'm probably biased, but I'm come to love everything I've read so far by Terry Pratchett, so perhaps my 5 stars should be taken with the proverbial "grain of salt". Still, I enjoyed this one immensely. The Discworld books sometimes have series within the overall series that follow a group of characters. This book is the 2nd in what I call the "Guards" series, following the Night Watch of Ankh-Morpork which is led by Sam Vimes. <br/>After the events of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/64216.Guards__Guards___Discworld___8__City_Watch__1_' title='Guards! Guards! (Discworld, #8; City Watch #1) by Terry Pratchett' rel='nofollow noopener'>Guards! Guards!</a> the Watch is being expanded, both</span>
  <span id="freeText365325837735762315" style="display:none">I'm probably biased, but I'm come to love everything I've read so far by Terry Pratchett, so perhaps my 5 stars should be taken with the proverbial "grain of salt". Still, I enjoyed this one immensely. The Discworld books sometimes have series within the overall series that follow a group of characters. This book is the 2nd in what I call the "Guards" series, following the Night Watch of Ankh-Morpork which is led by Sam Vimes. <br />After the events of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/64216.Guards__Guards___Discworld___8__City_Watch__1_" title="Guards! Guards! (Discworld, #8; City Watch #1) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Guards! Guards!</a> the Watch is being expanded, both in number and in ethnicity. Affirmative action of a sort has come to the Watch, and the new hires include trolls, dwarves, and werewolves, among others. Sam Vimes is planning on retiring from the Watch after his upcoming wedding, and a series of mysterious murders is occuring with an apparently new type of weapon, which fires metal pellets through the air. <br />I recommend reading the book Guards, Guards first if you can, but like most all of the Disworld series that I've read, this one can stand alone or even be your first foray into the Discworld if this is the only book available at the moment. I definitely urge anyone who enjoys fantasy, adventure, humor, satire and a touch of hard boiled mystery to check this one out asap. </span>
  <a data-text-id="365325837735762315" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10042500088230069796">The characters in this novel were superb, the plot and its ending was wholesome but not idealistic, and the narration was hilarious. I'm not sentimental about the series yet, but I bet I'll get there eventually. Man, Pratchett is something else. I can't think of an author I've read last that was able to comment on society, provide fleshed-out characters and a good plot, and present it all neatly with great humor. Maybe I just haven't been reading enough?</span>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer2792399028" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer16050463191436712179">A great story and funny too. The main character, Corporal Carrot, believes in the inherent goodness in all creatures and comes out of this story as the hero, closely followed by all members of the watch. The dialogue is excellent and the antagonists are beautifully portrayed, even Havelock Vetinari the Patrician. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer989791167" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17149357984846272018">2013 July 4<br/><br/>Pratchett can write a novel about integration and politics and gun control that keeps one amused and engrossed and thoroughly engaged the whole time. I enjoyed it so much that as soon as I was finished I purchased the next Discworld book I hadn't yet read, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/884288.Interesting_Times__Discworld___17__Rincewind__5_' title='Interesting Times (Discworld, #17; Rincewind #5) by Terry Pratchett' rel='nofollow noopener'>Interesting Times</a>, to start immediately.<br/><br/>And as many Pratchett books as I have read and loved at this point, I still find it hard to say why they're so great. There is always plot, often more than enough for several books. In this ca</span>
  <span id="freeText17149357984846272018" style="display:none">2013 July 4<br /><br />Pratchett can write a novel about integration and politics and gun control that keeps one amused and engrossed and thoroughly engaged the whole time. I enjoyed it so much that as soon as I was finished I purchased the next Discworld book I hadn't yet read, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/884288.Interesting_Times__Discworld___17__Rincewind__5_" title="Interesting Times (Discworld, #17; Rincewind #5) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Interesting Times</a>, to start immediately.<br /><br />And as many Pratchett books as I have read and loved at this point, I still find it hard to say why they're so great. There is always plot, often more than enough for several books. In this case there is a murder to solve and also the struggle of one man who's always been a cop and poorish to find a place for himself when he marries the wealthiest woman in town. And although are both compelling stories, I don't suggest you read his books to find out what happens. Without being at all precious or lyrical, the important thing about a Pratchett book is that you are being <b>told</b> a story. In the way that some actors could bring drama to the a reading of the phone book, Pratchett brings humor and insight to everything he writes, probably including his grocery lists. It's the literary equivalent of screwball comedy with everyone running about and delivering lots of dialogue very quickly and the leading characters are all quite clever.<br /><br />I bought it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17149357984846272018" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText18289685431861424896" style="display:none">This is the second Discworld book featuring the Night Watch, though it is okay to start here. You’ll just get suspicious looks from everyone.<br /><br />The Night Watch has added some new recruits, thanks to 90s-style political correctness and affirmative action. They find a dead body and find themselves unsure how to investigate.<br /><i><br />Murder was in fact a fairly uncommon event in Ankh-Morpork, but there were a lot of suicides. Walking in the night-time alleyways of The Shades was suicide. Asking for a short in a dwarf bar was suicide. Saying “Got rocks in your head?” to a troll was suicide. You could commit suicide very easily, if you weren’t careful.<br /></i><br />This story has a solid plot and plenty of Discworld humor and is plenty of fun. A lot of the “Moving Pictures” characters show up again here. The jokes are very pun-heavy:<br /><i><br />He was said to have the body of a twenty-five year old, although no-one knew where he kept it. The point was that everyone else had someone, even if in Nobby’s case it was probably against their will.<br /><br />“One minute walking along, the next minute dead. Why?”<br />THINK OF IT BEING MORE… DIMENSIONALLY DISADVANTAGED.<br /><br />“I believe in reincarnation,” [Bjorn] said.<br />I KNOW.<br />“I tried to live a good life. Does that help?”<br />THAT’S NOT UP TO ME. Death coughed. OF COURSE… SINCE YOU BELIEVE IN REINCARNATION… YOU’LL BE BJORN AGAIN.”<br /></i><br /><a href="https://audreytheeditor.blogspot.com/" rel="nofollow noopener"> Book Blog</a></span>

        <a title="Richard" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Richard" href="/user/show/8386404-richard">Richard</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780552140287" href="/book/show/833425.Men_at_Arms">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8386404-richard?shelf=comedy">comedy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8386404-richard?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1260983560" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11533024723249231690">8.5/10<br/><br/>I'm going to say that to date this is the best Discworld novel I've read. The characters are well rounded with a blend of people from the last novel developing further and new additions adding to humour and conflict in equal measure. The plot is well written with plenty of twists and turns and not until near the end did things tie up nicely making it a well driven read. <br/><br/>The layers to this novel were quite something, on the surface this is a murder plot but underneath it is littered with po</span>
  <span id="freeText11533024723249231690" style="display:none">8.5/10<br /><br />I'm going to say that to date this is the best Discworld novel I've read. The characters are well rounded with a blend of people from the last novel developing further and new additions adding to humour and conflict in equal measure. The plot is well written with plenty of twists and turns and not until near the end did things tie up nicely making it a well driven read. <br /><br />The layers to this novel were quite something, on the surface this is a murder plot but underneath it is littered with politics, racism, the influence a gun/gonne has over a person when in their possession, and the techniques of profiling suspects by the Police/Watch. All in a 350ish page comedy/fantasy!!!<br /><br />Saying all that, this is by no means a heavy novel. It's a Discworld novel so it flows really well and the style of writing is whimsical often littered with jokes and humorous interactions. It's truly a satirical novel, Pratchett is often at his best (or so it seems) when he is critiquing our world through the stupidity of the Discworld folk.<br /><br />If the sub-series of The Watch novels keeps on with this trend then I think I can firmly say that I will be hooked on the whole Discworld series and look to read them all. Vimes and co are becoming a fine unit and well worth reading about, I look forward to their next adventure.<br /><br />If you like this try: "Going Postal" by Terry Pratchett</span>
  <a data-text-id="11533024723249231690" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5917681-lady-clementina-ffinch-ffarowmore?shelf=humour">humour</a>,

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5917681-lady-clementina-ffinch-ffarowmore?shelf=terry-pratchett">terry-pratchett</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5917681-lady-clementina-ffinch-ffarowmore?shelf=citywatch">citywatch</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5917681-lady-clementina-ffinch-ffarowmore?shelf=discworld">discworld</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5917681-lady-clementina-ffinch-ffarowmore?shelf=british">british</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5917681-lady-clementina-ffinch-ffarowmore?shelf=dogs">dogs</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5917681-lady-clementina-ffinch-ffarowmore?shelf=dog">dog</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5917681-lady-clementina-ffinch-ffarowmore?shelf=animals">animals</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2460327341" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5664884591529954402">#17 of the Discworld Books and the second of the Citywatch ones. As usual, plenty is happening in Ankh-Morpork in this one. The Night Watch has new recruits, a troll, a dwarf (even though Carrot is one too :)), and a very pretty young woman, Constable Angua (who happens to have another special qualification), all part of an affirmative action plan, initiated by the Patrician. Carrot is now Corporal, in-charge, of them all. On the other side, Captain Vimes is preparing for his wedding to Lady Syb</span>
  <span id="freeText5664884591529954402" style="display:none">#17 of the Discworld Books and the second of the Citywatch ones. As usual, plenty is happening in Ankh-Morpork in this one. The Night Watch has new recruits, a troll, a dwarf (even though Carrot is one too :)), and a very pretty young woman, Constable Angua (who happens to have another special qualification), all part of an affirmative action plan, initiated by the Patrician. Carrot is now Corporal, in-charge, of them all. On the other side, Captain Vimes is preparing for his wedding to Lady Sybil Ramkin, protector of dragons, who we met in <i>Guards Guards</i>. And on yet another side, impoverished nobleman, and member of the assassin’s guild Edward D’Eath is disgruntled, in fact positively displeased with the Patrician’s rule, and wishes for the days of monarchy to be restored in Ankh-Morpork. Then strange events begin to happen, there is an explosion, bodies are found, on different days and different places, from the Clown’s guild, the beggar’s guild—with the assassin’s guild not claiming official responsibility for it, and there is also a mysterious theft with no clue as to <i>what</i> was stolen. Vimes wants to get to the bottom of it, as does Carrot, but the Patrician seems to want them not to investigate, or does he?<br /><br />This was a very entertaining, action-packed, and really enjoyable instalment for me. From the Discworld books I’ve read so far (not so many as yet), Guards Guards was among my favourites, and this one I enjoyed even more so. I loved the humour and writing as always [In the allusions department, this time, I don’t think I fared very well, not really picking any up (p.s: Just remembered an Oscar Wilde one).]. The plot had an unexpected twist or two and there was a proper mystery/whodunit element, which of course, I loved. Carrot was pretty surprising in this one, and definitely proved more than once that simple and trusting certainly does not mean stupid—in fact it could give one insights that, well, ‘intelligent’ people may not pick up on. As far as Vimes went, I felt the Patrician’s plan ended up backfiring a little. One aspect that stood out to me in this one was the social side of things—the issues of diversity, representation, people refusing to simply understand other people, very much reflecting the world we inhabit today. And in its fun way, it was able to deliver the message that if we really get to know each other, we might begin to understand and like each other, even, instead of simply being intent on destruction (a small soppy element in the story though I could have done without). I also thought his dogs/wolves ideas pretty interesting, and on the lighter side, liked the doggie character Gaspode. This took me a little while to get into (my fault I think, reading when much too tired) but once I did I thoroughly enjoyed myself. Can’t wait to get started on the next one. Four and a half stars.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5664884591529954402" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_844699154" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/844699154" />
    <a title="David Sarkies" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5856247-david-sarkies"><img alt="David Sarkies" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1470027282p2/5856247.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/844699154?book_show_action=true">Feb 04, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David Sarkies" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David Sarkies" href="/user/show/5856247-david-sarkies">David Sarkies</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780552140287" href="/book/show/833425.Men_at_Arms">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5856247-david-sarkies?shelf=comedy">comedy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer844699154" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10916221974498630808"><b>The Nightwatch Returns</b><br/>13 February 2014<br/><br/>	Terry Pratchett is now taking aim at the detective fiction in his gonne sights in one of the most amusing Discworld books that I have read to date (though that is a bit of an exaggeration, but this book does sit up there with the best of the series). We now return to the antics of the nightwatch and discover that there have been some promotions (though poor Noddy is not among the ones who have been promoted). The Patrician has also decided to embrace the id</span>
  <span id="freeText10916221974498630808" style="display:none"><b>The Nightwatch Returns</b><br />13 February 2014<br /><br />	Terry Pratchett is now taking aim at the detective fiction in his gonne sights in one of the most amusing Discworld books that I have read to date (though that is a bit of an exaggeration, but this book does sit up there with the best of the series). We now return to the antics of the nightwatch and discover that there have been some promotions (though poor Noddy is not among the ones who have been promoted). The Patrician has also decided to embrace the idea of affirmative action, so he encourages the watch to bring in some new recruits, including Detritus the Troll (who is famous for being the bouncer at the Broken Drum), Cuddy the dwarf, and Angua, though we are not sure if it is because she is a woman <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[or a werewolf <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>.<br /><br />	Alongside the antics of Detritus, Cuddy, and Angua, there is also a pretty impressive mystery (which involves a gun, or more precisely, a gonne) which has gone missing from the Assassins Guild, but let us first deal with Detritus and Cuddy. One thing that we learn about the Discworld in this book is that trolls and dwarves basically hate each other and wonder around in gangs beating each other up. However Detritus and Cuddy are forced to put aside their differences and work together (which they end up doing quite successfully because they are no longer a dwarf and a troll but members of the watch).<br /><br />	As for the mystery, we are first introduced to a rather intriguing assassin known as Edward d'Earth (I actually know of a person with such a last name), who was at one stage a member of a noble family but has fallen on hard times since the king was removed and the Patrician put in his place. However, due to some research, he uncovers the true heir to the throne and seeks to replace the Patrician with a new king, but this peters out pretty quickly because d'Eath ends up going missing pretty close to the beginning of the book. However Vimes comes to learn of some mysterious deaths which he decides to investigate (and we have his superior stepping in, as can be expected in your typical crime novel, and telling him to stop investigating, and then disbanding the watch because Vimes refuses to do so).<br /><br />	It is interesting Pratchett's take on the gonne because he paints it in the same way that he paints other technological innovations (such as film in Moving Pictures): it has a this magical effect upon society that irrevocably changes it. The funny thing with Discworld is that society is actually resistant to change, so we have these inventions having an almost magical effect upon those who are exposed to it to try to force this change along, and this is change that is usually for the worse. There is also something very seductive about the gun, sort of like the power that its possession gives the owner – the ability to be able to kill at great distances. <br /><br />	The interesting thing about Ankh-Morpork is that it exists purely due to the status quo, though one does seem to get more of a sense of a fairy-tale world than a pure fantasy world that one tends to expect from most fantasy novels (but then again Discworld is not like most fantasy worlds): a medieval European setting with wizards and dragons running around to make things interesting. Granted Discworld has its fare share of wizards, dragons, and a cornucopia of other caricatures, but there seems to an essence of modernism within this world, as if the thoughts and attitudes of the characters are more modern in scope than most fantasy novels. Then again this is not surprising since what Pratchett is writing satire as opposed to pure fantasy, and in many cases it is much easier to criticise society, and some of society's sacred cows, by shifting the setting away from the modern world and placing it in a fictional setting, whether it be fantasy, as in the case of Pratchett, or science-fiction, as in the case of writers like <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4.Douglas_Adams" title="Douglas Adams" rel="nofollow noopener">Douglas Adams</a> or <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/40119.Grant_Naylor" title="Grant Naylor" rel="nofollow noopener">Grant Naylor</a>.<br /><br />	That does not mean that the writings are going to be immune to criticism or outrage, as this tends to happen when writings start to demonstrate the absurdity of some of our sacred cows (such as religion in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/64217.Pyramids__Discworld___7_" title="Pyramids (Discworld, #7) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Pyramids</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/34484.Small_Gods__Discworld___13_" title="Small Gods (Discworld, #13) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Small Gods</a>, and gun ownership in the case of Men at Arms). Mind you, I have not heard anywhere near as much criticism being levelled against Pratchett as has been levelled against some other writers (such as Scorsesee in regards to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8737.The_Last_Temptation_of_Christ" title="The Last Temptation of Christ by Nikos Kazantzakis" rel="nofollow noopener">The Last Temptation of Christ</a>). I suspect that Pratchett has become something of a sacred cow himself (in the same way that Star Trek and Star Wars are also sacred cows) in that if one even thinks of writing a bad review of one of his books you are going to get someone complaining about how you are being unfair, don't understand, or simply (which is a huge assumption) haven't read the book (because, seriously, how could anybody even think of writing a bad review if they had actually read the book).</span>
  <a data-text-id="10916221974498630808" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer168812692" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer2841451196024912522">Barely a year has passed since the last idiot in Ankh-Morpork thought they could unleash an unholy hell to help them overthrow the great tyrant ruler of their city state only to find themselves meeting the grinning face of the feline loving Death of the Disc sooner than anticipated and somebody is at it again, only this time with the help of a 'gonne.' Vimes is off getting married and Carrot is inheriting a watch embracing equal opportunities, including the evolution of Detritus the troll in to </span>
  <span id="freeText2841451196024912522" style="display:none">Barely a year has passed since the last idiot in Ankh-Morpork thought they could unleash an unholy hell to help them overthrow the great tyrant ruler of their city state only to find themselves meeting the grinning face of the feline loving Death of the Disc sooner than anticipated and somebody is at it again, only this time with the help of a 'gonne.' Vimes is off getting married and Carrot is inheriting a watch embracing equal opportunities, including the evolution of Detritus the troll in to series regular and a rather lovely female with whom Carrot would like to go for long walks around the city, plus of course there are assorted Librarians, wonder dogs, clowns, assasins, dwarfs, Nobbys, trolls and gargoyles. It's a wonderful time as always when The Watch are out to play, it just feels like Pratchett knew how to write these stories better than most others, enabling him to play a bit more with genre conventions, characterisation and satire. Of course the homage to The Third Man can't escape mention, you'll have the image of Orson Welles and that wonderful zither music in your head for days afterwards I'm sure.</span>
